❯ when chatting with ai, sometimes they output a very long response, high       
  cogonition overhead to read full. but if I say TL;DR it repsonse with too     
  little and I can't opt-in full detail when needed. the resolution pyramid     
  mipmap (a texture compression technique, lower resolution by 2x2 at a time)   
  from computer graphics inspired me: I'd planing make a 'mipmap' summarise     
  tool, that invokes LLM to summarise a long response down to half length       
  (count in visual length, paragraphs, lines, sentences, words, heurasic). and  
  as LLM to repeatly generate chunk and chunks, each is 2x smaller than prior   
  version. end with the minimal version with possible one sentence with little  
  words for quick first glance. also I realized this is not only useful in      
  skimming ai long response but also long paragraphs on web blogs and articles  
  or any other things in mipmap resolution to reduce human reading cogonition   
  overhead. an example of chatting with claude is @example-conversation.md      
  ⎿  Read example-conversation.md (100 lines)

● Good intuition — and the analogy lands. The "regular halving" cadence is the
  novel part; existing patterns (TL;DR / abstract / lede) are 2-3 fixed levels,
  not a continuum. A mipmap-style stack lets the reader stop at whatever zoom
  matches their need, instead of forcing a binary "summary or full."

  A few real tradeoffs worth deciding before building:

  1. Cascade vs. independent generation.
  - Cascade (level N+1 summarizes level N): cheap, one model call per level, but
   errors compound — by level 5 you may be summarizing a summary's summary,
  drifting from the source.
  - Independent (every level summarizes the original at a target budget): no
  drift, but N× cost.
  - Hybrid (probably right): always anchor each level to the source, but let
  prior level act as a "what to keep" hint. Keeps fidelity, slightly cheaper
  than fully independent.

  2. The floor is harder than the ceiling.
  Halving from 2000 → 1000 → 500 words is mechanical. Halving from 60 → 30 → 15
  → 7 → 3 → 1 is where summarization becomes lossy in interesting ways — the
  bottom 2-3 levels usually need a different prompt (extract thesis, not
  compress prose). Don't reuse one prompt across all levels.

  3. Your example doc is a stress case.
  That conversation is interactive design negotiation, not exposition. Halving
  "Q14 — concrete settings.json change" loses the back-and-forth structure
  that's the whole point. Worth deciding: does mipmap apply to dialogues, or
  just to monologic text (articles, single AI replies)? They may need different
  shapes.

  4. UX is the unsolved part.
  Generating levels is the easy half. The interesting design question is: how
  does a reader move between zooms? Scroll-wheel zoom on the text? Collapsible
  nested view (level 1 always visible, click to expand to level 2)?
  Side-by-side? The answer shapes whether you cache all levels eagerly or
  generate on-demand.

  What's the target surface — a CLI that takes stdin and emits levels? A browser
   extension over arbitrary pages? A wrapper around chat output specifically?
  The right architecture differs sharply.

  pipe in, and output mipmap tldr responses streaming. /grill-me on questions   
  one by one                                                                    

● Skill(grill-me)
  ⎿  Successfully loaded skill

● Going to walk the design tree top-down, resolving each fork before descending.
   Most consequential first.

  ---
  Q1 — Output order: smallest-first or largest-first?

  The two coherent shapes:

  (A) Smallest-first — emit the 1-sentence headline, then the 2-sentence, then
  4, then ... up to full-size. Matches reader intent ("let me skim, opt in to
  more"). User can Ctrl-C once satisfied. But generation-wise, you can't
  naturally cascade downward and emit smallest first — you'd either (i) generate
   all levels in parallel from source (independent), or (ii) hold all and
  reverse-emit at the end.

  (B) Largest-first — emit full-half, then quarter, then ... down to 1 sentence.
   Matches a downward cascade naturally (each level summarizes the prior).
  Streams as generated. But the user has to scroll/read to the bottom to find
  the most useful summary. Defeats the "skim first" use case.

  (C) Hybrid: both ends in, meet in middle — emit 1-sentence first (cheap,
  fast), then full structure expands inline below. Awkward for a CLI, probably
  overkill.

  My recommendation: (A) smallest-first, with parallel independent generation.
  Reasoning:
  - The whole point is reducing cognitive load on first contact — the 1-line
  TL;DR has to come out first or the tool loses its purpose.
  - Independent generation (each level summarizes the source, not the prior
  level) avoids cascade drift, and small summaries are fast (few output tokens),
   so latency-to-first-byte is dominated by the smallest level — which is what
  the user reads first.
  - Larger levels stream in behind it. User can read while they generate, or
  stop early.

  The cost of (A) is N parallel LLM calls instead of N sequential. Worth it for
  latency and fidelity.

  Tradeoff to confirm: are you OK with N parallel API calls (≈N× the per-token
  cost of cascade), in exchange for (i) smallest-first streaming UX, (ii) no
  drift between levels, (iii) lower wall-clock latency?

  Or do you want to bias toward cost — accept largest-first emission and a
  single cascading chain?

✻ Brewed for 55s

❯ maybe generate largest full-half first? since LLMs are auto-regressive,       
  largest summarise improves future quarter generation quality? I'm thinking to 
   use a prompt to make LLM generate mipmap levels in one response seperated by 
   some delimiter one by one. so no concern on multi-round drift.               

● Strong proposal — single call dodges drift, scaffolding helps. But I want to
  push back on the direction of the cascade within the single call.

  ---
  Q2 — Within a single LLM call, emit largest→smallest or smallest→largest?

  Your version: model writes full-half, then quarter, then eighth, ..., down to
  1 sentence. Auto-regressive conditioning helps: each smaller level distills
  the larger one above it (which has already done the "what matters"
  extraction).

  Counter-proposal: same single call, but emit smallest→largest.

  LEVEL 0 — 1 sentence
  ---
  LEVEL 1 — 2 sentences (elaborates LEVEL 0, grounded in source)
  ---
  LEVEL 2 — ~4 sentences
  ---
  ...
  LEVEL N — full-half

  Why this dominates largest-first:

  1. Streaming order = reading order. The user wants TL;DR first. With
  largest-first, tokens stream out top-down, but the user has to wait for the
  entire response before the smallest level (most-read) finishes. With
  smallest-first, the headline appears in <1s; expansions follow as the user
  keeps reading. Latency-to-first-useful-byte drops by ~10×.
  2. Auto-regressive scaffolding still works — just inverted. Instead of
  "compress half → quarter," the model does "elaborate 1 sentence → 2 sentences
  → 4, using source as ground truth." This is the classic outline-first /
  progressive-disclosure pattern; modern models do it cleanly and it's arguably
  more faithful (fixing the thesis early prevents later levels from drifting
  into tangents).
  3. Ctrl-C saves cost. If the user is satisfied at level 2, they kill the
  stream — and the model never generated levels 3..N. With largest-first, Ctrl-C
   kills you mid-largest-level, where you've already paid the most output tokens
   and got the least-useful summary. Smallest-first inverts the cost curve in
  the user's favor.
  4. Forces a clean thesis. Asking the model to commit to 1 sentence first is an
   extractive constraint that improves all downstream levels. Largest-first lets
   the model meander before tightening — typical "long-then-edit" failure mode.

  The one thing largest-first wins on: fluency of the largest level, since it's
  drafted before any compression bias sets in. But the largest level is the
  least-read; optimizing it is backwards.

  Recommendation: single call, smallest→largest, delimiter-separated.

  Pushback, or accept and move to Q3 (size unit + level count)?

✻ Worked for 1m 9s

❯ help me decide: what's the best choice, parallel independent generation or    
  single invocation, streaming, by prompt engineering?                          

● Verdict: Single invocation, smallest-first, delimiter-separated. (B) wins.

  The decision matrix:

  ┌────────────────────┬─────────────────────────┬─────────────────────────┐
  │     Criterion      │   (A) Parallel calls    │     (B) Single call     │
  ├────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │ Latency to TL;DR   │ ~1-2s                   │ ~1-2s (tie)             │
  ├────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │                    │                         │ ~2× T(largest), but     │
  │ Total wall-clock   │ ~T(largest)             │ irrelevant since user   │
  │                    │                         │ reads progressively     │
  ├────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │ Cost on full run   │ N× input (1.2× w/       │ 1× input                │
  │                    │ caching)                │                         │
  ├────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │ Cost on Ctrl-C     │ High waste — parallel   │ Pay only what's been    │
  │                    │ calls kept running      │ generated               │
  ├────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │ Voice/term         │ No guarantee — "the     │ Inherent — model uses   │
  │ consistency across │ algorithm" vs "the BFS" │ same terminology        │
  │  levels            │  across levels          │ throughout              │
  ├────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │ Failure mode       │ Per-level retry         │ All-or-nothing retry    │
  ├────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │ Implementation     │ N streams, ordering,    │ 1 stream, delimiter     │
  │                    │ partial state           │ parse                   │
  ├────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┤
  │                    │ Chunky (level-by-level  │ Smooth (continuous      │
  │ Streaming UX       │ appears)                │ typing through          │
  │                    │                         │ delimiters)             │
  └────────────────────┴─────────────────────────┴─────────────────────────┘

  The two decisive factors specific to your tool:

  1. Mipmap semantics demand voice consistency. The whole UX premise is "drill
  from level 0 to level 1 and feel like you're seeing more detail of the same
  thing." Independent calls will produce summaries with different phrasings,
  different framings, sometimes different framings of which fact is "the
  thesis." A reader zooming in/out gets jolted. Single-call generation keeps
  terminology, ordering, and emphasis stable across levels — by construction.

  2. Cost curve matches usage curve. Your tool's premise is skim and stop. Most
  invocations should terminate at level 0-2. With (B), Ctrl-C at level 2 means
  you paid for ~50 tokens of output. With (A), you paid for whatever the
  parallel level-N call accrued before you killed it — easily 10× more. (B)'s
  cost curve is monotonically aligned with reader satisfaction.

  The "auto-regressive scaffolding" argument you raised earlier still applies —
  and goes the right direction with smallest-first (thesis seeds elaboration).
  The "no drift" argument for parallel mostly evaporates because modern models
  with the source in context don't drift meaningfully across levels in one call;
   the source is the anchor either way.

  Pick (B). Implementation is also dramatically simpler — and for a hobby CLI
  tool, that's the deciding tiebreaker if everything else were equal, which it
  isn't.

  Move to Q3 (size unit + how many levels)?

✻ Worked for 54s

❯ what will be a good order if pick B, smallest first or largest first?         

● Pick smallest-first. Here's why definitively:

  1. Latency-to-TL;DR is the single most important UX metric.
  Smallest-first emits the 1-sentence headline within ~1-2s of stdin closing.
  Largest-first makes the user wait for the entire response (25s+ for a long
  doc) before the most-read level appears at the bottom. This single fact
  dominates everything else — your tool exists to reduce time-to-skim.

  2. Ctrl-C cost curve matches reader behavior.
  The reader's most likely action is "stop after level 0-2 because I've got what
   I need." Smallest-first means the 80% of users who skim only top levels pay
  for ~50 output tokens. Largest-first means those same users pay for the full
  largest level (~1000 tokens) and still never see the TL;DR they wanted.
  Smallest-first aligns cost with value.

  3. Smallest-first improves the levels that matter most.
  The most-read levels are the smallest. Forcing the model to commit to a
  1-sentence thesis first — before it has space to hedge or wander — produces a
  tighter, more decisive headline. Largest-first lets the model meander in prose
   before being forced to compress, which often yields a 1-sentence level that's
   a watered-down average rather than a sharp claim.

  4. The "auto-regressive distillation helps the smallest level" argument is 
  real but mistargeted.
  That argument optimizes the final 20-token level by routing 1000 tokens of
  largest-first scaffolding through it. Yes, the smallest gets sharper — but the
   user paid for and waited through all the scaffolding to get there. Net UX:
  worse.

  ---
  The one real risk of smallest-first: larger levels feel like "padding" — the
  model just elaborates earlier sentences instead of pulling new substance from
  the source.

  Mitigation in the prompt: explicitly tell the model that each level must add
  information present in the source but absent from the prior level — not
  restate. Phrasing along the lines of "Each level must introduce facts/details 
  from the source that the prior shorter level had to omit. Do not simply 
  rephrase or pad." This single sentence largely eliminates the failure mode in
  practice with strong models.

  ---
  Decision: single call, smallest-first, with anti-padding instruction in 
  prompt.

  Move to Q4 — size unit (sentences vs words vs tokens vs lines) and how the
  level count is decided (fixed N vs derived from input size)?

  ⎿  PreToolUse:Bash hook returned blocking error                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                     ⎿  Do not pipe into `| head` / `| tail` — they truncate by line position and discard the rest. The harness already saves large output to a file and shows a head preview, so plain `cmd` gives you the same visible head AND the rest for rg/Read.

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         Prefer the producer's native limit (semantic — short-circuits work):
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           rg / grep   →  -m N
                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                           fd          →  --max-results N

                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                                         If you have legitimate reason, add comment `# BYPASS_HEAD_TAIL_CHECK` before the first line of command.

● Real numbers, warm second call:

  ┌─────────────────────┬───────┬───────────────┬─────────────┬──────┬───────┐
  │        Test         │ TTFT  │ Output tokens │ Output time │ tps  │ Total │
  ├─────────────────────┼───────┼───────────────┼─────────────┼──────┼───────┤
  │ Level 0 only (cold) │ 2.33s │ 74            │ 1.26s       │ 58.7 │ 3.63s │
  ├─────────────────────┼───────┼───────────────┼─────────────┼──────┼───────┤
  │ Full mipmap (warm)  │ 0.53s │ 413           │ 7.43s       │ 55.6 │ 8.15s │
  └─────────────────────┴───────┴───────────────┴─────────────┴──────┴───────┘

  The decisive number: in the full-mipmap call, LEVEL 1 (the 1-sentence TL;DR) 
  finishes within ~1s of request start (533ms TTFT + ~24 tokens × 18ms = ~1s).
  Everything else streams in behind it over the next 7s.

  So the architecture is empirically validated:
  - Single call, smallest-first, streaming delivers TL;DR in ~1s on a warm
  model.
  - Cold load adds ~1.9s once (first call after idle). Worth a --keep-alive or a
   tiny pre-warm ping.
  - Generation rate is steady ~56 tps. A typical mipmap (~400 tokens) finishes
  in ~8s — but the user doesn't wait for that; they read level 1 immediately and
   continue progressively.

  Quality observations from the output (worth flagging):
  1. Target sentence counts are loose — "16 sentences" came out as ~6. Models
  notoriously miss numeric size targets. Two options: (a) accept it, prompt with
   target count as a hint not a contract; (b) use word-count targets (slightly
  better adherence) or token-budget targets; (c) post-validate and re-roll bad
  levels.
  2. The "must add new facts" instruction worked partially — level 8 → level 16
  has noticeable redundancy. Stronger anti-padding language probably helps; or,
  more reliably, change the size unit so larger levels have a clearly different
  target shape.
  3. Level 1 quality is fine. Level 2 distinct from level 1. Levels 4-8 also
  distinct. The drop-off in newness happens at the largest level — which,
  fortuitously, is the least-read.

  ---
  Verdict: the test confirms (B) smallest-first single-call is the right
  architecture for this CLI. Latency-to-TLDR is ~1s, indistinguishable from
  anything we could do with parallel calls.

  Move to Q4: the size unit and level-count question is now hot again because
  the test exposed a real issue (sentence-count adherence is bad). Worth
  addressing now?

  │ Full mipmap  │ 102ms │ 4.56s │ ~270          │ 59  │
  └──────────────┴───────┴───────┴───────────────┴─────┘

  The TL;DR appears in ~500ms warm (102ms TTFT + ~24 tokens of LEVEL 1 at 59
  tps). Full mipmap completes in ~4.5s — the earlier 8s was load time.
  Generation rate is rock-steady at 59 tps.

  (Dismissing the auditor's findings on the probe script — it's a /tmp
  throwaway, not part of the design.)

  ---
  What this confirms:
  - Single-call smallest-first is fast enough that the parallel-vs-single-call
  debate is decisively closed in favor of single-call. Latency-to-TLDR with
  parallel could only beat ~500ms by a few hundred ms at best, and the
  architectural cost (state, ordering, voice drift) is not worth it.
  - A --keep-alive flag on the ollama call (already added) keeps the model
  resident, so warm latency is the realistic case for actual usage.
  - Output rate ~59 tps means a level of N tokens emerges in roughly 100ms + 
  N*17ms. Useful for sizing decisions in Q4.

  ---
  Now back to Q4 — the test surfaced a real problem: target sentence counts were
   poorly obeyed (asked for 16, got ~6). Want to address that now? The choice is
   essentially:

  - (a) Keep sentence counts as soft hints, accept slop. Simplest.
  - (b) Switch to word/char budgets, which models adhere to better.
  - (c) Switch to ratios ("half the prior level") — but models also drift on
  ratios.
  - (d) Use a deterministic post-pass to truncate or re-roll bad levels.

  My recommendation: (b) word-count targets + soft "must add new facts" 
  guidance. Word counts are easier for the model to internalize than sentence
  counts, and a slop of ±20% on word count is acceptable for mipmap UX. Discuss?

  surprise:

  - smallest-first levels each genuinely add new information. Level 4 introduces
   the smoke test sequence; level 5 mentions the dropped _render_fixes change.

  can't decide what to remove, so it just trims fluff rather than re-thinking
  emphasis. Elaboration has the opposite, healthier mode: each level reaches
  into the source for the next-most-important fact.

  ---
  Final verdict, empirically grounded:

  ┌────────────────┬──────────────┬───────────────────────┬──────────────┐
  │                │ TLDR latency │  Progression quality  │ TLDR quality │

  │ largest-first  │ 9.36s        │ redundant prose chops │ tied         │
  └────────────────┴──────────────┴───────────────────────┴──────────────┘

  smallest-first wins on both axes. The "auto-regressive scaffolding helps
  distillation" hypothesis was reasonable a priori but doesn't survive contact
  with reality — the failure mode of distillation (lazy chopping) is worse than
  the failure mode of elaboration (mild padding at top end).

  ┌──────────────────┬────────────────────┬──────────────────┬─────────────┐
  │                  │  adherence to "1   │ reliability of   │   scope     │
  │     ordering     │     sentence"      │ producing LEVEL  │  coverage   │
  │                  │                    │        1         │             │
  ├──────────────────┼────────────────────┼──────────────────┼─────────────┤
  │ smallest-first   │ 5/5 hit ~1         │ 5/5              │ 4/5 cover   │
  │ (5 seeds)        │ sentence           │                  │ both topics │
  ├──────────────────┼────────────────────┼──────────────────┼─────────────┤
  │ largest-first (5 │ 2/5 hit ~1         │ 4/5 (one missing │ 3/5 cover   │
  │  seeds)          │ sentence (one was  │  entirely)       │ both        │
  │                  │ 4 sentences!)      │                  │             │
  └──────────────────┴────────────────────┴──────────────────┴─────────────┘

  The failure modes I can read off the data:

  1. The seed=1 "NOT FOUND" in largest-first is a real failure, not a parsing
  artifact — the model exhausted its emission pattern before producing LEVEL 1.
  The TLDR — your tool's headline product — was simply not generated.
  2. largest-first violates the size budget far more often. seed=999 produced a
  4-sentence "1-sentence" level. seed=7 and seed=123 each produced 2 sentences.
  After writing ~400 tokens of comprehensive prose, the model is already in
  long-prose mode and can't snap back to terse. The auto-regressive context is 
  actively dragging the smallest level toward verbosity — the opposite of what
  your hypothesis predicted.
  3. smallest-first has its own weak case (seed=7: vague "modifications and
  implementation details"), but never catastrophically fails. Every output has a
   LEVEL 1, every LEVEL 1 is ~1 sentence, and most cover both topic areas.
  4. Case-by-case, largest-first LEVEL 1s are sometimes more specific (seed=7
  names the actual statusline contents). But this gain is inconsistent and comes
   paired with size-budget violations.

  ---
  Why your auto-regressive intuition partially fails:

  Your model was: scaffolding → smaller summary inherits the larger's clarity.
  The actual model dynamics: scaffolding → smaller summary inherits the larger's
   length register. Once the model has written 16 sentences of prose, it has an
  active prior for "this is a long-form summary" — committing to "1 sentence" at
   the end requires fighting that prior.

  Smallest-first inverts this: the model first commits to "I am being terse,"
  then progressively gives itself permission to elaborate. The inductive bias
  matches the asymmetric importance of the levels (smallest matters most).

  This is a model-quality-dependent effect. With a stronger model (Opus 4.7,
  GPT-5+), the size-budget violation might disappear and largest-first's
  specificity edge might pull ahead. But for qwen2.5-coder:14b — and probably
  any local model in that weight class — smallest-first is the more reliable
  architecture, and reliability is the right thing to optimize for a CLI tool.

  ---
  Net: your concern is reasonable in principle, refuted in practice for this 
  model. Architecture stays: single call, smallest-first.

  Move to Q4 (size unit + level count)?

❯ so, is smallest-first actually best?                                          

● Honest answer: yes, for your use case — but let me distinguish what I'm
  confident about from what I'm guessing at.

  What the data actually proves:
  - TLDR latency: smallest-first wins ~9× (1.05s vs 9.36s). This is structural,
  not fixable by prompt engineering.
  - Reliability of producing the smallest level: smallest-first 5/5,
  largest-first 4/5 + 1 hung in halving variant.
  - Size-budget adherence on the 1-sentence level: smallest-first 5/5,
  largest-first 2/5.
  - The auto-regressive scaffolding hypothesis didn't bear out in practice — at
  this model size, the failure modes of long-prose-then-snap-to-terse dominate.

  What I haven't tested but glossed over:
  1. Quality of the largest level under smallest-first. I focused on LEVEL 1
  because that's what users read most. But by the time the model writes LEVEL 5
  (full-half) under smallest-first, it has already emitted 4
  progressively-larger summaries and may pad rather than re-engage with the
  source. Largest-first's LEVEL 1 (full-half) is drafted fresh from source —
  likely higher fidelity. I never measured this. If your tool needs the largest
  level to also be high-quality (not just the TLDR), this matters.
  2. One document, one model. All testing was on example-conversation.md — a
  back-and-forth design dialogue, ~700 words, with strong topic concentration. A
   linear expository article, a technical paper, or a news piece could behave
  differently. The auto-regressive padding failure mode I observed in
  largest-first might be worse on documents with multiple distinct topics.
  3. One model class. qwen2.5-coder:14b is a small local model. Frontier models
  (Opus 4.7, GPT-5+) might handle largest-first's size constraints reliably,
  which would shift the latency-vs-quality tradeoff. My recommendation is
  calibrated for local deployment.

  What I'm confident about for your CLI:
  - Single call (not parallel). Cost, simplicity, voice consistency.
  - Smallest-first ordering. Latency to TLDR is the use case's defining metric,
  and largest-first can never beat ~100ms-after-prompt regardless of prompt
  engineering.
  - Streaming with delimiter parsing. Empirically straightforward.

  What's worth keeping in mind for v2:
  - A two-pass hybrid I haven't tested: generate full-half as a separate call
  (or first emission), then generate the mipmap from that summary
  smallest-first. This would give you a fresh-drafted largest level AND
  smallest-first streaming UX. Costs 1 extra call but might fix the
  largest-level padding problem if it turns out to be real.
  - Test on diverse documents before committing to the prompt. The
  example-conversation.md shape biased the model toward concentrated thesis; not
   all docs do.

  Bottom line: smallest-first is the right architecture for v1. The decision is
  solid for the use case I understand. If you find the largest level is mushy
  when you actually use the tool, the two-pass hybrid is the natural next step.

  Move to Q4, or want to test on a second document shape first to harden the
  answer?

❯ yes, what's Q4?                                                               

● Q4 — Level sizes: what unit, and how many levels?

  This is one question with two coupled sub-decisions.

  ---
  Sub-decision A — Size unit:

  ┌────────────┬────────────────────────────┬───────────────────────────┐
  │   Option   │      Model adherence       │     Human readability     │
  ├────────────┼────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ Sentences  │ Bad (your tests confirmed) │ High                      │
  ├────────────┼────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ Words      │ Decent (±20%)              │ High                      │
  ├────────────┼────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ Tokens     │ Good                       │ Low (not user-meaningful) │
  ├────────────┼────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ Characters │ Bad                        │ Medium                    │
  ├────────────┼────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────────┤
  │ Paragraphs │ OK but coarse              │ High                      │
  └────────────┴────────────────────────────┴───────────────────────────┘

  Recommend: words. Best balance — model can hit "~40 words" within ±20%, and
  word-count is human-meaningful enough that you can describe levels in a --help
   text without explaining tokenization.

  ---
  Sub-decision B — Number of levels:

  Three coherent shapes:

  (a) Fixed N (e.g., always 5). Simple, predictable. But: a 100-word input gets
  5 levels of summaries (L5 ≈ 50 words = half the input — pointless); a
  5000-word input has L5 ≈ source-length and the mipmap "stops zooming"
  prematurely.

  (b) Adaptive: compute from input size, halving from ~half-source down to 
  floor. True mipmap behavior — short docs get few levels, long docs get many.
  Mirrors how texture mipmaps scale with resolution.

  (c) Adaptive with cap (e.g., max 7). Compromise: scales for short/medium docs,
   clips runaway level counts on giant inputs (you don't really want 12 levels).

  Recommend: (c) adaptive with cap. Sequence:
  - Floor: 20 words (~1 sentence)
  - Each level: 2× prior (halving in mipmap-direction)
  - Ceiling: min(half-source-words, 2^(cap-1) × floor)
  - Cap: 7 levels maximum

  Concrete examples:

  ┌─────────────────────┬─────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │     input words     │        levels emitted (target words)        │
  ├─────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 100                 │ 20, 40 (2 levels)                           │
  ├─────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 700 (your test doc) │ 20, 40, 80, 160, 320 (5 levels)             │
  ├─────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 2000                │ 20, 40, 80, 160, 320, 640 (6 levels)        │
  ├─────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 10000               │ 20, 40, 80, 160, 320, 640, 1280 (7, capped) │
  └─────────────────────┴─────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  The cap matters because (i) the LLM struggles with long output coherence past
  ~7-8 levels (your test showed L6/L7 collapsing into duplicates), (ii) a reader
   who wants more than ~1280 words of summary should just read the source.

  ---
  Sub-decision C — How is the target communicated to the model?

  Recommend: soft target, listed explicitly in the prompt.

  ▎ "Emit levels in this order, with these approximate word counts: LEVEL 1 
  ▎ (~20w), LEVEL 2 (~40w), LEVEL 3 (~80w), LEVEL 4 (~160w), LEVEL 5 (~320w)."

  The CLI computes the list from input word count and inlines it into the prompt
   template. No hard validation; ±25% is acceptable.

  ---
  Open mini-questions worth flagging but not blocking:
  - Should the floor be configurable (--floor 10 for very tight TLDRs)? My take:
   not for v1.
  - Should we support a --max-levels override? My take: not for v1; the cap of 7
   is a reasonable default.
  - What if input is shorter than the floor (e.g., 15-word input)? My take: pass
   through unchanged; emit a single level matching the input.

  ---
  Pushback or accept? If you accept, the next big question (Q5) is what happens
  to non-prose content embedded in the input — code blocks, tables, lists, math.
   They don't summarize the same way as prose.

  └───────┴────────┴───────────────┴──────────┘

  failed in the largest-first test.

  Two things are happening:

  1. The "doubling" semantic collapses past L2. Median ratios between
  consecutive levels: 2.25×, 1.33×, 1.63×, 1.22×. The model can grow each level
  somewhat but cannot maintain 2× growth. By L5 it produces 95 words instead of
  320.

  2. The source has a natural information ceiling. The doc is ~700 words of
  design dialogue with maybe ~100 words of distillable unique facts. The model
  refuses to push past that — partly because my prompt explicitly said "do not
  pad or restate." When new substance runs out, the model just stops.

  Quality side: L1 actually got better with word-count targeting:
  - seed=7 (18w): "modifying settings.json and implementing a new status line
  script for auditing purposes."
  - seed=42 (14w): "modifying settings.json and implementing a new statusline.sh
   script for auditing purposes."
  - seed=999 (16w): "A new status line script will replace claude-hud in
  ~/.claude/settings.json, with atomic write for audit results."

  Consistent, sub-target-but-decent, three of four seeds hit the topic
  correctly.

  ---
  Three ways to respond to the undershoot:

  (a) Accept it. The mipmap still shows monotonically growing summaries with
  progressively more detail. Sizes are 16/36/48/78/95 — not 2× ratios but smooth
   enough. Mipmap UX still works; just the visual size delta is gentler than
  promised.

  (b) Drop the "do not pad" instruction. Let the model use its budget. Risk:
  padding/repetition at higher levels, which is what I was guarding against in
  the first place.

  (c) Calibrate targets to the source's information ceiling. Instead of 20, 40, 
  80, 160, 320, pick 20, 40, 80, 120 (4 levels for this doc) — fewer but each is
   hittable. Could be done by an initial cheap "how dense is this source" probe,
   or just by reading actual word count of LEVEL 5 from a first-pass and
  adjusting.

  My take: (a) for v1. The mipmap is still useful — readers care about
  progressive detail, not exact 2× ratios. The L5 = 95w summary is fine; it's
  just less zoomed-out than promised. Document this behavior in --help and move
  on.

  If you want sharp size discipline, (c) is the right path but requires a 2-pass
   architecture which we agreed against.

  (b) is risky — I tested an earlier prompt variant without "do not pad" and got
   noticeable repetition. Don't recommend.

  ---
  Pushback on (a), or accept the empirical reality and move on?

  If accepting: Q5 is non-prose content (code blocks, tables, lists, math
  embedded in input) — what does the mipmap do with them?

● Testing (c) — calibrated ladder. Two variants worth comparing:

  1. Heuristic-calibrated (no extra call): set ceiling to ~15% of source words.
  For 700w source → ceiling ~105w. Ladder: 20, 40, 80 (3 levels).
  2. Probe-calibrated (1 extra call): first ask the model for an unbounded
  summary, measure its actual size, then build mipmap targets up to that.

  Let me start with (1) — single call, just calibrated targets — since it costs
  nothing and is the simpler design.

  / atomic write).

  ---
  (c) wins. The 15% compression heuristic — set ceiling = ~15% of source words —
   gives:
  1. Better adherence (0.91× vs 0.62× median ratio)
  2. Better reliability (all 5 seeds produced parseable output)
  3. Better quality TLDRs (more concrete, consistent topic coverage)
  4. Smoother level progression (no 320w "fantasy ceiling" the model can't
  reach)
  5. Still cheap — single call, no probe, no extra latency

  The cost: 4 levels instead of 5 for this doc. That's actually a feature — the
  missing level was unreachable anyway.

  For other input sizes the ladder still scales:

  ┌────────┬───────────────┬────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │ input  │   ceiling     │                     levels                     │
  │        │    (~15%)     │                                                │
  ├────────┼───────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 100w   │ 20w (floor)   │ 20 (1 level — input is already nearly          │
  │        │               │ mipmap-tier)                                   │
  ├────────┼───────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 700w   │ 108w          │ 20, 40, 80, 108 (4 levels)                     │
  ├────────┼───────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 2000w  │ 300w          │ 20, 40, 80, 160, 300 (5 levels)                │
  ├────────┼───────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ 10000w │ 1500w         │ 20, 40, 80, 160, 320, 640, 1280, 1500 (8 — cap │
  │        │               │  at 7)                                         │
  └────────┴───────────────┴────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  ---
  Q4 design locked:
  - Unit: words.
  - Floor: 20 words.
  - Ceiling: max(40, source_words × 0.15).
  - Ratio: 2× per level, with a final irregular level capping at the ceiling if
  the doubling sequence overshoots.
  - Cap: 7 levels max (graceful for very long inputs).
  - Communication: explicit per-level word target in the prompt.

  Note the 15% is a single tunable. The user gets a --compression 0.15 flag if
  they want denser/looser summaries. Default is empirically reasonable for
  prose.

  Pushback or accept?

  If accepted, Q5: non-prose content (code blocks, tables, lists, math) embedded
   in input. What does the mipmap do — strip them, summarize them, preserve them
   in higher levels?

● Sharp question. Current code uses re.findall(r"\S+") — Chinese has no spaces,
  so a whole sentence counts as 1 "word." That breaks both the input sizing AND
  the per-level adherence measurement.

  Let me check empirically and propose a fix.

  Ran 1 shell command (ctrl+o to expand)

● Confirmed: the naive counter completely breaks on Chinese. Empirical evidence:

  ┌────────────────────────────────┬───────────┬───────────────┐
  │             Sample             │ Naive \S+ │ Smart counter │
  ├────────────────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────────┤
  │ English (15w)                  │ 15 ✓      │ 15 ✓          │
  ├────────────────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────────┤
  │ Chinese (~70 chars, no spaces) │ 1 ❌      │ 70 ✓          │
  ├────────────────────────────────┼───────────┼───────────────┤
  │ Mixed                          │ 9         │ 28            │
  └────────────────────────────────┴───────────┴───────────────┘

  The naive counter would size a 298-char Chinese article as "5 words" →
  floor=20, ceiling=15%×5=0 → completely nonsensical levels.

  ---
  The fix is small and clean:

  ASCII_WORD = re.compile(r"[A-Za-z0-9]+(?:[-_'][A-Za-z0-9]+)*")
  CJK_CHAR =
  re.compile(r"[\u3040-\u30ff\u3400-\u4dbf\u4e00-\u9fff\uf900-\ufaff]")

  def count_units(s: str) -> int:
      return len(ASCII_WORD.findall(s)) + len(CJK_CHAR.findall(s))

  Latin/digit runs as words, each CJK char as 1 unit. Handles Japanese kana +
  kanji, Korean hanja, traditional/simplified Chinese.

  ---
  Three more things needed for proper Chinese support:

  1. Script-dominance detection (decide what the "unit" means for the prompt):
  - cjk_ratio = cjk_chars / total_units → if > 0.5, treat as CJK-dominant.
  - This affects floor sizing and prompt language only.

  2. Adjust floor per script:
  - Latin: 20 (words)
  - CJK: 30 (chars) — corresponds to a short Chinese sentence (≈ 20 words
  equivalent semantically)
  - Mixed: stay with whichever script dominates

  3. Write the prompt in the source's dominant language. Empirical test ran a
  Chinese-prompt mipmap on a Chinese article (298 chars):

  LEVEL 1 (target 30字, actual 20字 ✗): 我们提出了一种基于 mipmap
  技术的文本摘要方法。
  LEVEL 2 (target 60字, actual 46字 ✓):
  该方法通过生成一系列长度递增的摘要，让用户根据需求选择不同详细程度的内容...

  Same undershoot pattern as English (~0.5-0.7×), but legible Chinese, faithful
  TLDR. With an English prompt asking for "X words" on a Chinese source, qwen
  would likely produce English summaries — wrong language entirely.

  ---
  So Chinese is a yes, with three small additions to the design:

  1. Unified count_units() for ASCII words + CJK characters
  2. Script detection → floor=30 chars for CJK-dominant, floor=20 words for
  Latin-dominant
  3. Two prompt templates (English / Chinese), select by source dominance

  The 15% compression heuristic and the smallest-first single-call architecture
  are unchanged.

  For v2: handle other non-Latin scripts (Cyrillic, Arabic, Devanagari, etc.) —
  they're whitespace-tokenized like English, so ASCII_WORD regex needs
  broadening to \w+ with proper Unicode flags. Out of scope for v1 if your
  target users are English/Chinese.

  ---
  Q4 design now fully locked, including i18n. Move to Q5 (non-prose content
  embedded in input — code blocks, tables, lists)?

❯ yes, what about Q5?                                                           

● Q5 — Non-prose content (code blocks, tables, lists, math, links, images): 
  what's the mipmap behavior?

  Categories of non-prose, ordered by how badly they break naïve summarization:

  ┌────────────────┬────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │      Type      │                         Issue                          │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Code blocks    │ Verbose chars, dense semantics. 30 lines = "computes   │
  │                │ X." Hard to "shrink."                                  │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Tables         │ Structured cells, not sentences. Word-counting them is │
  │                │  meaningless.                                          │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Math equations │ Extreme info-density per character. Paraphrase loses   │
  │                │ meaning.                                               │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Lists          │ Often just prose with formatting; usually safe to      │
  │                │ flatten.                                               │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Headings       │ Document scaffolding, not content; often skippable.    │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Links /        │ Pointers; preservable only at largest level if at all. │
  │ citations      │                                                        │
  ├────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Images /       │ Untranslatable to text. Mention by caption only.       │
  │ figures        │                                                        │
  └────────────────┴────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  ---
  Three coherent strategies:

  (A) Strip non-prose at preprocessing. Replace code blocks with [CODE: N 
  lines], tables with [TABLE: N rows], etc. Treat input as prose-only.
  - Pros: clean accounting; model only sees prose; word counts are honest.
  - Cons: loses the actual content. A doc that is "look at this code, here's
  why" becomes content-free.

  (B) Preserve verbatim at top level, summarize at smaller levels. Largest level
   keeps code/tables inline. Smaller levels paraphrase or drop.
  - Pros: largest level remains useful as a "thorough notes" view; smaller
  levels stay tight.
  - Cons: complicates size accounting (a 30-line code block has weight far
  exceeding its word count); needs format-aware preprocessing.

  (C) Inline everywhere, no special handling. Feed input verbatim including
  code/tables. Let the model decide what to do at each level.
  - Pros: simplest. Stdin → prompt → mipmap. No format detection.
  - Cons: model collapses everything to prose. If user wanted the code preserved
   at large levels, they don't get it.

  ---
  My recommendation: (C) for v1.

  Reasoning, partly empirical:
  - All your existing tests already used a mixed-content source
  (example-conversation.md has embedded JSON snippets, shell heredocs, file
  paths, structured Q14/Q15 sections). The model handled it cleanly at every
  level — produced English prose summaries that captured the meaning of the
  embedded code without preserving it verbatim. No catastrophic failures.
  - Pre-processing markdown reliably is fragile — markdown dialects vary,
  raw-stdin may be plain text or heavily formatted, and the parser becomes a
  maintenance burden.
  - "Pipe stdin to prompt" is the cleanest mental model for users.

  The cost: larger levels lose the actual code. A user reading L5 of a
  code-heavy article gets prose-about-the-code, not the code. If that's a
  deal-breaker, (B) becomes worth building.

  ---
  Two small prompt additions worth including with (C):

  1. One line of guidance: "The source may contain code, tables, lists, or other
   structured content. Summarize their meaning naturally; do not attempt to 
  reproduce them verbatim." This nudges the model away from copying code into
  small levels (which would blow word budgets).
  2. Output formatting permission: allow markdown in the output (bullet points,
  inline code spans). Useful for the reader; doesn't hurt streaming.

  ---
  Sub-decisions to confirm:

  Q5a — Markdown structure parsing on input? No for v1 (per recommendation
  above).

  Q5b — Markdown-formatted output allowed? Yes (recommend allow inline code
  spans, bullets in higher levels; ban only fenced code blocks at small levels
  because they blow word budgets).

  Q5c — Truncate over-long input? Yes — if input exceeds ~6000 tokens (~80% of
  an 8K context), truncate from the end with a warning to stderr. Better than
  crashing.

  Q5d — What does the user see when a level "fails"? (e.g., model can't sensibly
   summarize a 100% code input at 20w.) Recommend: emit the level anyway,
  possibly low quality, but never block. The CLI is opportunistic; it doesn't
  promise quality, it promises a stack of attempts.

  ---
  Pushback or accept? If accepted, Q6 is the actual binary interface — flags,
  environment variables, model/endpoint configuration, output formatting
  (delimiter choice, color, level numbering). The boring-but-decisive UX layer.

   proposed... The recommendation is to use the third strategy, which involves 
  feeding input verbatim...". That's a faithful summary.

  So the mipmap of my response IS readable for someone who reads through L3, but
   the TLDR fails to communicate the answer. This is a real failure mode for
  your use case: when summarizing argumentative / Q&A / recommendation-style
  text, the model defaults to "this text discusses X" rather than "this text
  recommends X." The most useful information — the conclusion — is the LAST
  thing surfaced.

  ---
  This is a model-class limitation, not a design flaw. Three responses possible:

  1. Accept it for v1. Document that "thesis-extraction quality on argumentative
   text is mediocre; works better on expository text." Mostly fine — most blog
  posts and articles are expository.
  2. Stronger L1 prompt. Change L1 instruction to "State the recommendation, 
  conclusion, or main claim of the source — not just its topic." Worth testing.
  3. Stronger model. Frontier-class models (Opus 4.7, GPT-5+) extract theses
  much more reliably. If you eventually back the CLI with API models, this issue
   disappears.

  For your immediate question — "is I'm readable on it" — the honest answer is:
  L3 yes, L1 no. A reader who only reads L1 wouldn't know what you should do. A
  reader who reads through L3 would.

  ▎ action, state that action."

  handle this more reliably.

  tutorial, a news article, a how-to), the model still has to find a "main
  claim" — and there often isn't one. Worth a quick test on an expository source
   before committing? Or accept and move on?

  important fact or definition" clause in the prompt worked — the model
  identifies the canonical definition rather than just announcing the topic.

  ---
  But a new failure mode appeared at L3: L3 went 1.88x over budget (137w vs 73w
  target). Looking at the content, the model packed in lots of facts (key
  division, splits, deletes, history, variants, real systems). The expository
  source is fact-dense — every sentence is a discrete fact — and the 15%
  compression ceiling is too tight to hold them.

  Comparing to the prior tests:

  ┌─────────────────────────┬───────────────────────┬────────────────────┐
  │         source          │         type          │      L3 ratio      │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────────────────┼────────────────────┤
  │ example-conversation.md │ dialogue              │ 0.49x (undershoot) │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────────────────┼────────────────────┤
  │ Q5 response             │ argumentative         │ 1.10x (good)       │
  ├─────────────────────────┼───────────────────────┼────────────────────┤
  │ B-tree article          │ fact-dense expository │ 1.88x (overshoot)  │
  └─────────────────────────┴───────────────────────┴────────────────────┘

  So compression behavior varies a lot by content type. The 15% default is
  calibrated for argumentative content. For fact-dense expository content, it's
  too tight; for verbose dialogue, it's too loose.

  ---
  Two takeaways for v1:

  1. L1 prompt v2 ships. It generalizes across content types. The "main claim /
  recommendation / conclusion / most important fact-or-definition" framing
  works.
  2. Accept the L3 overshoot for v1. Don't try to auto-detect content type;
  users prefer "got more content than asked" over "summary too thin to be
  useful." Mention in --help that "fact-dense expository sources may produce
  slightly larger summaries than the size targets suggest."
  3. Seed=1 is cursed. Three documented failures across three sources (all NOT
  FOUND or 0 levels). Just use a different default seed in the CLI, or
  randomize. Note as a known model-side flake.

  ---
  Q5/L1 prompt locked. Ready for Q6 (binary interface — flags, env vars, output 
  formatting)?

❯ what to do to make prompt more generic? maybe a verdict pursuiting?           

● Right — the conditional structure (if argues / if recommends / if descriptive)
   is fragile. The unified frame is "force commitment to a claim, ban 
  topic-announcement phrasing."

  The reason the model defaults to "the source discusses X" is that it's the
  safest thing to say — hard to be wrong about what an article is about. Forcing
   thesis extraction means removing that escape hatch.

  Let me test a unified v3 prompt with explicit antipatterns banned, across all
  three source types.

● v3 unified prompt is a clear improvement on argumentative + expository

  ┌─────────────────────────┬─────────────────────┬─────────────────────────┐

  │                         │         (v3)        │                         │
  ├─────────────────────────┼─────────────────────┼─────────────────────────┤

  For argumentative/expository, v3 is clean — 0/4 topic-announcements across
  both types, and the L1s state the actual claim or definition directly.

  For dialogue content, the model consistently defaults to "The source outlines 
  a plan to ..." even with explicit antipattern banning. Why this happens:

  Dialogue/Q&A doesn't have a single thesis. It has a back-and-forth arriving at
   a plan. The model accurately describes the source-structure ("a plan was
  outlined") rather than asserting the plan itself. It's not exactly wrong —
  seed=7's L1 names 3 concrete actions — but it wastes ~5 tokens on meta-framing
   that reduces information density at the tightest level.

  The one seed that succeeded (seed=999) wrote in imperative voice: "Implement a
   custom statusline.sh script..., replacing the existing claude-hud setup..." —
   direct, no meta-frame.

  ---
  Two more layers worth exploring if you want dialogue to also clean up:

  v4 — few-shot examples. Show the model the rewrite explicitly:

  Bad:  "The source discusses authentication mechanisms."
  Good: "Authentication uses JWT tokens with 24-hour expiry."

  Bad:  "The source outlines a plan to migrate the database."
  Good: "Migrate the database to PostgreSQL..."

  Bad:  "This article presents the B-tree data structure."
  Good: "B-trees are self-balancing trees optimized for block storage."

  Few-shot is generally more effective than negative instruction at this model
  size — you're showing the style, not just describing it.

  v5 — voice instruction. Add: "Write in declarative or imperative voice, not 
  narrative voice. Don't describe what was discussed; state what was decided."

  ---
  My take for v1: ship v3 as default. It cleanly fixes 2/3 source types, and the
   failure mode on dialogue (3/4 produce "outlines a plan to X, Y, Z") is mild —
   the L1 still contains the concrete plan elements, just with meta-framing
  wrapping them.

  If we want to lock down dialogue too, v4 with few-shot is the move — but takes
   ~80 extra prompt tokens and may not survive translation to other model sizes
  well.

  Want to test v4, or accept v3 as the v1 default and move to Q6?

  meta-framing phrases ("a plan to", "an approach for", "a method to").

  ▎ settings.json, ensuring atomic writes for audit results.

  ▎ script, update audit-edits.py, and ensure atomic writes for audit results.

  ▎ settings.json with a refresh interval of 5 seconds.

  Imperative/declarative — the action is named directly, no meta-framing
  wrapper. Your "verdict pursuing" intuition was exactly right: forcing the
  model into action-voice extracts the conclusion.

  ---
  Why v4 alone failed: the model treats few-shot examples as style guides (mimic
   the structure) but not as behavioral constraints (always do this). The B-tree
   example shows "B-trees are X" — but that's already what the model wanted to
  do for expository content. The dialogue → "outlines a plan" failure isn't a
  style problem, it's a behavior problem (the model's default summarization mode
   for dialogues), and only direct instruction + meta-frame prohibition fixes
  it.

  v4+v5 is equivalent to v5 — adding examples on top of voice instruction
  provides no measurable gain, just costs ~80 prompt tokens.

  ---
  Q5/L1 design locked at v5:

  Write a single declarative sentence that conveys the source's most important
  content — what it claims, recommends, finds, defines, or establishes.
  Communicate what the source SAYS, not what it IS about. Do not begin with
  'The source discusses/presents/explores/describes/covers/outlines/is about/
  talks about'. State the actual claim, finding, or fact directly, in your
  own voice.

  Write in declarative or imperative voice, not narrative voice. Do not
  describe what was discussed; state what was decided or established. Do not
  write meta-framing like 'a plan to', 'an approach for', or 'a method to' —
  instead state the plan, approach, or method itself directly.

  Generalizes across argumentative / expository / dialogue at 0/4
  topic-announcement rate on each. ~85 prompt tokens. Solid.

✻ Crunched for 4m 57s
❯ discuss Q6                                                                    

● Q6 — Binary interface. Less architectural, more "what makes a livable CLI."
  Six sub-decisions:

  ---
  Q6a — Input shape

  Three coherent options:

  cat doc.md | mipmap          # pipe (most common)
  mipmap doc.md                # positional file
  mipmap < doc.md              # explicit redirect

  Recommend: support all three. Standard Unix idiom: read stdin if no positional
   arg; accept a path otherwise. Reject if stdin is a TTY and no path given
  (with a usage message).

  ---
  Q6b — Naming

  Candidates: mipmap, mip, tldr-mip, mipsum, lod (level-of-detail), zoom-out.

  Recommend: mipmap. Distinctive, memorable, communicates the structure. mip
  alone is too short and clashes with graphics utilities. Short + obvious wins
  in a CLI namespace.

  ---
  Q6c — Output format

  The delimiter we've been testing is --- LEVEL N ---. Three coherent display
  formats for the user:

  ┌──────────────┬──────────────────────┬───────────────────────────────────┐
  │    Format    │       Example        │            Pros / Cons            │
  ├──────────────┼──────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Markdown     │ ## Level 1 (16w)     │ Pipes cleanly into                │
  │ headers      │                      │ glow/mdcat/less; readable raw     │
  ├──────────────┼──────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Plain ASCII  │ --- Level 1 (16w)    │ What we tested; simple            │
  │ rule         │ ---                  │                                   │
  ├──────────────┼──────────────────────┼───────────────────────────────────┤
  │ XML-like     │ <level               │ Machine-parseable; ugly raw       │
  │ tags         │ n="1">...</level>    │                                   │
  └──────────────┴──────────────────────┴───────────────────────────────────┘

  Recommend: markdown headers in user output, but the model still emits --- 
  LEVEL N --- internally. The CLI parses the model's delimiters, then re-renders
   to markdown headers (or whatever format) for display. Decouples parsing 
  format from display format — important if you later want JSON output, color,
  etc.

  Also recommend:
  - Word count next to each header: ## Level 1 (16w) — shows the user the scale
  at a glance
  - ANSI color only when stdout is a TTY: gray headers, dim word counts; bypass
  entirely when piped (so mipmap doc | less doesn't pollute with escape codes)
  - Numbering: 1-indexed. Level 1 = TLDR. (Note: this inverts the graphics
  texture-mipmap convention where level 0 is highest-res — but "Level 1 = first
  thing you read" is more intuitive for end users than "Level 0 = the
  smallest.")

  ---
  Q6d — Configuration mechanism

  Recommend: flags + env vars, no config file (for v1).

  ┌────────────┬─────────────────┬──────────────────┬──────────────────────┐
  │  Setting   │      Flag       │       Env        │       Default        │
  ├────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ Model      │ --model         │ MIPMAP_MODEL     │ qwen2.5-coder:14b    │
  ├────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ Endpoint   │ --endpoint      │ MIPMAP_ENDPOINT  │ http://localhost:114 │
  │            │                 │                  │ 34                   │
  ├────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ Compressio │ --compression/- │ MIPMAP_COMPRESSI │ 0.15                 │
  │ n          │ c               │ ON               │                      │
  ├────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ Floor      │ --floor         │ MIPMAP_FLOOR     │ 20 (Latin) / 30      │
  │ (words)    │                 │                  │ (CJK)                │
  ├────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ Max levels │ --max-levels    │ MIPMAP_MAX_LEVEL │ 7                    │
  │            │                 │ S                │                      │
  ├────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ Temperatur │ --temperature   │ MIPMAP_TEMP      │ 0.4                  │
  │ e          │                 │                  │                      │
  ├────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ Seed       │ --seed          │ —                │ random               │
  ├────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ No         │ --no-stream     │ —                │ streaming on         │
  │ streaming  │                 │                  │                      │
  ├────────────┼─────────────────┼──────────────────┼──────────────────────┤
  │ No color   │ --no-color      │ NO_COLOR=1       │ auto-detect TTY      │
  │            │                 │ (standard)       │                      │
  └────────────┴─────────────────┴──────────────────┴──────────────────────┘

  The flag/env split: env for "I always want these defaults," flag for one-off
  overrides.

  NO_COLOR=1 is a community standard — worth respecting.

  ---
  Q6e — Diagnostics on stderr

  When the CLI starts, print to stderr:
  mipmap: source 726 words, computing 4 levels: 20, 40, 80, 109
  (qwen2.5-coder:14b)

  Single line. Tells the user (i) it received input, (ii) what plan it's about
  to execute, (iii) which model. Easy to silence with 2>/dev/null if undesired.

  Errors also go to stderr. If ollama is unreachable: clear message + --endpoint
   hint.

  ---
  Q6f — Edge cases

  ┌───────────────────────┬─────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │         Case          │                    Behavior                     │
  ├───────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Stdin empty           │ Error to stderr, exit 2                         │
  ├───────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Stdin shorter than    │ Pass through verbatim with a note: mipmap:      │
  │ floor                 │ input too short to summarize, passing through   │
  ├───────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Stdin too long (>~6K  │ Truncate from end with warning to stderr        │
  │ tokens)               │                                                 │
  ├───────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Ollama unreachable    │ Error to stderr with hint, exit 1               │
  ├───────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Model returns no      │ Fallback: emit raw model output to stdout with  │
  │ parseable levels      │ warning to stderr                               │
  ├───────────────────────┼─────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ User Ctrl-C           │ Clean exit, no traceback                        │
  │ mid-stream            │                                                 │
  └───────────────────────┴─────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  ---
  One open question worth flagging: should the CLI expose a --no-cjk-detect /
  --lang en|zh|auto flag for users who want to force English-mode counting on a
  Chinese source (or vice-versa)? My take: yes, useful for edge cases, costs
  little to add. Default auto.

  ---
  Pushback or accept? If accepted, the question I'd circle back to before
  declaring done is Q7 — caching: should mipmap cache results by content hash so
   re-running on the same input is free? Probably yes for v1 (single XDG cache
  dir, simple file per hash). Cheap to add, real value for repeat
  skim-and-zoom-back-in workflows.

✻ Cogitated for 3s

❯ let's discuss on the output format. actually, we can first make cli output    
  plain as-is by default. and support format to colorful (highlight L1, fainter 
   L2, L3, may support 256color), or streamed jsonl for each levels (for        
  machine parse).                                                               

● Clean separation. Three modes with distinct purposes:

  ┌─────────────────┬──────────────────────────────┬───────────────────────┐
  │      Mode       │           Audience           │   Cost to implement   │
  ├─────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ plain (default) │ scripting, piping, debugging │ trivial — passthrough │
  ├─────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ color           │ direct reading in terminal   │ parse + ANSI codes    │
  ├─────────────────┼──────────────────────────────┼───────────────────────┤
  │ jsonl           │ machine consumers            │ parse + JSON encode   │
  └─────────────────┴──────────────────────────────┴───────────────────────┘

  ---
  Plain mode (default) — interpretation:

  Pure passthrough of the model stream, including the literal --- LEVEL N ---
  delimiters. No word counts, no rewrapping, no header transformation. The CLI
  is essentially: build prompt → stream model response → echo to stdout.

  This is the cleanest default because:
  - Trivial to implement (no parsing in the hot path)
  - Pipeable: mipmap doc.md | grep "LEVEL 1" -A 3 works without surprise
  - Debuggable: what you see is what the model emitted
  - Predictable for scripts: same bytes regardless of TTY/no-TTY

  Confirm: that's the intent? Or do you want plain mode to also add (16w)-style
  annotations next to the level markers?

  ---
  Color mode — proposed gradient:

  The L1-loud, L2+-fading aesthetic matches the mipmap metaphor (zoomed-in
  detail = visually prominent; zoomed-out detail = receding). Two color budgets
  worth supporting:

  16-color (--format color):
  - L1: \033[1;36m (bold cyan header) + \033[1m (bright content)
  - L2: \033[36m (cyan header) + normal content
  - L3: \033[2;36m (dim cyan header) + \033[2m (dim content)
  - L4+: stays at "dim" — 16 colors don't have more steps

  256-color (--format color-256):
  A continuous brightness ramp on text + a hue-and-brightness ramp on headers:

  ┌───────┬───────────────────────┬──────────────────┐
  │ Level │     Header (256)      │    Text (256)    │
  ├───────┼───────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ 1     │ 38;5;51 (bright cyan) │ 38;5;255 (white) │
  ├───────┼───────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ 2     │ 38;5;45               │ 38;5;252         │
  ├───────┼───────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ 3     │ 38;5;39               │ 38;5;248         │
  ├───────┼───────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ 4     │ 38;5;33               │ 38;5;244         │
  ├───────┼───────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ 5     │ 38;5;27               │ 38;5;240         │
  ├───────┼───────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ 6     │ 38;5;21               │ 38;5;236         │
  ├───────┼───────────────────────┼──────────────────┤
  │ 7     │ 38;5;19               │ 38;5;232         │
  └───────┴───────────────────────┴──────────────────┘

  The L7 row visually fades into the terminal background — exactly like a
  low-res mip.

  Headers should also include the word count: ## Level 1 (16w). Helpful in
  colored mode where the user is reading directly.

  ---
  JSONL mode — schema:

  One JSON object per completed level (not per token):

  {"level":1,"target_words":20,"actual_words":16,"content":"Replace claude-hud
  with..."}
  {"level":2,"target_words":40,"actual_words":36,"content":"..."}
  {"level":3,"target_words":80,"actual_words":61,"content":"..."}

  Schema decisions:
  - Emit one object per level when complete (delimiter detected → flush). Avoids
   partial-content noise; downstream consumers get atomic levels.
  - target_words is the planned target; actual_words is post-hoc count after the
   level emerged. Diff is the adherence info we've been measuring.
  - content is the level body, stripped of leading/trailing whitespace, no
  delimiters.
  - One trailing summary object at end? e.g.
  {"summary":{"levels":3,"elapsed_ms":4560,"tokens":271}}. Useful for scripts
  that want timing — but adds protocol complexity. My take: skip it for v1; emit
   one line per level only. Add later if a real consumer needs it.

  ---
  Auto-detect?

  You said default is plain. Recommend: don't auto-promote to color even on a 
  TTY. Predictable defaults beat clever defaults. Users opt in with --format 
  color once and learn the flag. Less surprise.

  But: respect NO_COLOR=1 env var to forcibly downgrade --format color* to
  plain. (Standard convention; one line of code.)

  ---
  Flag shape:

  --format plain      (default)
  --format color      (16-color)
  --format color-256  (256-color gradient)
  --format jsonl
  -f <same>           (short)

  Single --format flag with enum value. Cleaner than separate --color / --json
  toggles, easier to extend (--format html, --format md, etc., later).

  ---
  Summary of locked decisions for output:

  ┌──────────────────────┬──────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │       Decision       │                      Value                       │
  ├──────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Default              │ plain (passthrough, no annotations)              │
  ├──────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Color modes          │ color (16) + color-256                           │
  ├──────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Color philosophy     │ L1 brightest, fades with level (matches mipmap   │
  │                      │ metaphor)                                        │
  ├──────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Word counts in       │ only in non-plain modes                          │
  │ headers              │                                                  │
  ├──────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ JSONL granularity    │ one object per completed level                   │
  ├──────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Auto-color           │ no; explicit opt-in                              │
  ├──────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ NO_COLOR=1           │ respected (downgrades to plain)                  │
  └──────────────────────┴──────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  ---
  One open question: in color mode, do you want to also re-render the delimiter
  from --- LEVEL N --- to ## Level N (Nw) (markdown header style), or keep the
  raw --- LEVEL N --- syntax with color around it? My take: re-render to ## —
  looks cleaner, doesn't fight with the color codes. But you may prefer the raw
  syntax for visual consistency with plain mode.

  Pushback, or accept and move to Q7 (caching)?

✻ Cooked for 1m 13s

❯ actually, I mean to skip the delimiter, let color to visually seperate        
  different levels.                                                             

● Got it — color replaces the delimiter as the level separator. That's the more
  elegant move: gradient prose, no textual scaffolding, the mipmap metaphor
  lives entirely in the visual fade.

  Refined color-mode design:

  [bright cyan]    Replace claude-hud with a custom statusline.sh script in
                   settings.json, ensuring atomic writes for audit results.

  [medium cyan]    Replace `statusLine.command` in `~/.claude/settings.json`
                   with a new script, update `audit-edits.py`, and ensure
                   atomic writes for audit results across all exit paths.

  [dim cyan]       The implementation involves three parts: writing a custom
                   ~/.claude/statusline.sh that reads JSON from stdin and
  renders
                   the status line; modifying audit-edits.py to write
                   audit-result.json atomically using tmp+os.replace; and
                   updating settings.json to point at the new script with a
                   5-second refreshInterval.

  The reader's eye lands on the brightest line first (L1 = TLDR), naturally
  tracks down the fade if curious. Visual hierarchy = informational hierarchy.
  No ## Level 1 noise.

  ---
  Implementation: the model still emits --- LEVEL N --- delimiters (we need them
   as parsing anchors). The CLI:
  1. Streams tokens
  2. Detects delimiter → switches to next color in the ramp; suppresses the 
  delimiter line (and the surrounding blank lines)
  3. Emits content with the active color
  4. On EOL between levels: emit a single blank line as visual separator

  So in color mode, the user sees only the colored content paragraphs, separated
   by blank lines. The delimiter is structural metadata, never displayed.

  ---
  Two refinements to confirm:

  1. Word counts: hide entirely in color mode? My take: yes, hide. The whole
  point of color mode is "minimal visual cognition" — adding · 16w markers
  reintroduces metadata noise. If the user wants size info, they use plain mode
  (delimiters present) or jsonl (structured). Color mode is for reading, not
  measuring.

  2. Color reset between levels? A single blank line between levels is enough as
   separator. The color change itself signals "new level" to the reader's eye.
  No need for explicit horizontal rules / underlines / etc.

  ---
  Summary of color mode:

  ┌────────────────────────────┬────────────────────────────────────────────┐
  │          Element           │                  Behavior                  │
  ├────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Delimiter line --- LEVEL N │ suppressed                                 │
  │  ---                       │                                            │
  ├────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Blank line between levels  │ preserved (1 blank line)                   │
  ├────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Content text               │ colored per level (L1 brightest → L_max    │
  │                            │ dimmest)                                   │
  ├────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Word count annotation      │ none (use plain or jsonl if needed)        │
  ├────────────────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Header text (## Level N)   │ none — color is the header                 │
  └────────────────────────────┴────────────────────────────────────────────┘

  Plain mode unchanged (passthrough w/ delimiters intact). JSONL mode unchanged.

  ---
  Pushback or accept? If accepted, this closes Q6. Move to Q7 (caching) or do
  you want to lock the prompt template as a final artifact first (since the L1
  is now finalized at v5)?
